Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b05825/asset/images/medium/am-2018-05825f_0005.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b05825/asset/images/large/am-2018-05825f_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b05825&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Molybdenum disulfide (MoS<sub>2</sub>) has attracted extensive research interest as a fascinating anode for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) because of its high specific capacity of 670 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. However, unsatisfied cycling durability and poor rate performance are two barriers that hinder MoS<sub>2</sub> for practical application in SIBs. Herein, 3D interconnected MoS<sub>2</sub> with enlarged interlayer spacing epitaxially grown on 1D electrospinning carbon nanofibers (denoted as MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs) was prepared as a flexible anode for SIBs via <span class="smallcaps smallerCapital">l</span>-cysteine-assisted hydrothermal method. Benefitting from the C–O–Mo bonding between the CNFs and MoS<sub>2</sub> as well as the rational design with novel structure, including the well-retained 3D interconnected and conductive MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs networks and expanded (002) plane interlayer space, the flexible MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs electrode achieves a remarkable specific capacity (528 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>), superior rate performance (412 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>), and ultralong cycle life (over 600 cycles at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> with excellent Coulombic efficiencies exceeding 99%). The elaborate strategy developed in this work opens a new avenue to prepare highly improved energy storage materials, especially suitable for flexible electronics.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D26982%26pageCount%3D8%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DWei%2BLi%252C%2BRan%2BBi%252C%2BGuoxue%2BLiu%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D10%26issueNum%3D32%26contentID%3Dacsami.8b05825%26title%3D3D%2BInterconnected%2BMoS2%2Bwith%2BEnlarged%2BInterlayer%2BSpacing%2BGrown%2Bon%2BCarbon%2BNanofibers%2Bas%2Ba%2BFlexible%2BAnode%2BToward%2BSuperior%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D8%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D26989%26publicationDate%3DAugust%2B2018" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2018 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291493&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Carbon nanomaterials" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Carbon%20nanomaterials" class="article__tags__link">Carbon nanomaterials</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" class="article__tags__link">Electrodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291688&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Layered materials" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Layered%20materials" class="article__tags__link">Layered materials</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291009&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Nanofibers" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Nanofibers" class="article__tags__link">Nanofibers</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292522&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Two dimensional materials" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Two%20dimensional%20materials" class="article__tags__link">Two dimensional materials</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Molybdenum+Disulfide&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_molybdenum%20disulfide">molybdenum disulfide</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Enlarged+Interlayer+Spacing&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_enlarged%20interlayer%20spacing">enlarged interlayer spacing</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Carbon+Nanofibers&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_carbon%20nanofibers">carbon nanofibers</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Flexible&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_flexible">flexible</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">As an appealing alternative to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have drawn extensive research interest for large-scale application, due to the low cost, abundant availability of sodium resources as well as the suitable redox potential (<i>E</i><sub>Na+/Na</sub> = −2.71 V), and analogous energy mechanisms of SIBs to LIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3" aria-label="reference 1 to 3">(1−3)</a> MoS<sub>2</sub>-based materials have triggered increasing attention as an anode in SIBs owing to their unique two-dimensional (2D) structures and large interplanar spacing to enable larger Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction in the electrode. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4" aria-label="reference 4">(4)</a> MoS<sub>2</sub> crystallizes in a graphite-like anisotropy layered S–Mo–S sandwich structure via van der Waals interactions with a large interlayer spacing of about 6.2 Å. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5" aria-label="reference 5">(5)</a> The unique structure makes MoS<sub>2</sub> a perfect anode for both LIBs and SIBs with high capacity of 670 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11" aria-label="reference 6 to 11">(6−11)</a> Nevertheless, easy restacking and aggregating of these unique 2D structures and the low inherent electronic conductivity of MoS<sub>2</sub> would cause fast capacity fading and inferior rate performance. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12 ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12 ref13" aria-label="reference 12 and 13">(12,13)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Different strategies have been developed to mitigate the above-mentioned problems associating with MoS<sub>2</sub>-based anodes. The nanostructure design and hybridization with conductive materials such as carbon have been widely practiced. First, fabrication of rationally designed nanostructured MoS<sub>2</sub>, such as nanoflakes, nanosheets, and nano-MoS<sub>2</sub> with expanded interlayer spacing, can prevent the inferior structure change upon cycling and lead to enhanced electrochemical performance. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 14 to 17">(14−17)</a> For example, building 2D MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets on three-dimensional (3D) network structure could prevent the restacking and aggregation of MoS<sub>2</sub>, resulting in good electrochemical performance. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> Hu et al. expanded the (002) interlayer distance of MoS<sub>2</sub> nanoflowers from 0.63 to 0.69 nm, which would accelerate the reaction by exposing plenty of active sites, attaining a high discharge capacity (300 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>) and excellent rate performance (195 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> Another widely accepted strategy is to incorporate MoS<sub>2</sub> with conductive and elastic carbonaceous additives, including graphene, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20 ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref21" aria-label="reference 20 and 21">(20,21)</a> carbon nanofibers, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22 ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref23" aria-label="reference 22 and 23">(22,23)</a> and polymers, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> which could significantly enhance the electrical conductivity of MoS<sub>2</sub>-based materials and simultaneously buffer the structure change during cycling processes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25 ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25 ref26" aria-label="reference 25 and 26">(25,26)</a> Actually, combining the two mentioned approaches is a more effective way to boost the electrochemical properties of MoS<sub>2</sub>-based anodes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27 ref28 ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27 ref28 ref29" aria-label="reference 27 to 29">(27−29)</a> For example, Zhang et al. grew MoS<sub>2</sub> with enlarged (002) crystalline interplanar spacing on carbon nanotubes (CNTs) by a hydrothermal method. The 3D hierarchical MoS<sub>2</sub>/CNTs nanostructure could improve the conductivity, mechanical strength and hence strengthen the cycle stability. Meanwhile, the expanded interlayer spacing of MoS<sub>2</sub> could accelerate the kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation and create sufficient space to accommodate the inserted Na<sup>+</sup>. This integrated strategy leads to excellent electrochemical performance of a high specific capacity of 504.6 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup> over 100 cycles and 495.9 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 200 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> In spite of this progress, the rational design and synthesis of flexible MoS<sub>2</sub>-based materials toward flexible energy storage devices still remains a significant challenge.</div><div class="NLM_p">Apart from designing MoS<sub>2</sub>-based active materials, the fabrication of electrodes is another key factor for exploring advanced electrode materials for SIBs. Recently, developing flexible electrodes has been an urgent task to meet the ever-growing requirement of flexible electronics. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31 ref32 ref33 ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31 ref32 ref33 ref34" aria-label="reference 31 to 34">(31−34)</a> Flexible carbonaceous substrates, such as carbon nanofibers, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35 ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35 ref36" aria-label="reference 35 and 36">(35,36)</a> carbon paper, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> and graphene paper, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> have been largely employed as ideal current collectors to load active materials. Even though the reported flexible MoS<sub>2</sub>/flexible carbonaceous substrate composites exhibited enhanced SIBs performance, it still needs to be explored to enhance the chemical coupling between MoS<sub>2</sub> and flexible carbonaceous substrates. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23 ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref39" aria-label="reference 23 and 39">(23,39)</a> The well orientation and distribution of MoS<sub>2</sub> and flexible carbonaceous substrates in the composite with strong chemical coupling would reduce charge-transfer resistance and voltage hysteresis, allowing for rapid charge transfer and outstanding structural stability upon cycling. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40 ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40 ref41" aria-label="reference 40 and 41">(40,41)</a> Therefore, the strong chemical coupling between active MoS<sub>2</sub> and flexible carbonaceous substrates is of great importance.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Herein, we present the fabrication of 3D interconnected MoS<sub>2</sub> with enlarged interlayer spacing epitaxially grown on 1D electrospun carbon nanofibers (denoted as MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs) through an <span class="smallcaps smallerCapital">l</span>-cysteine-assisted hydrothermal method. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> Different from the &nbsp;previous&nbsp;reports, the MoS<sub>2</sub> and CNFs were strongly coupled via the C–O–Mo bonding. When tested as a flexible anode for SIBs, the MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs achieved a high discharge specific capacity (528 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>), excellent rate capability (412 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>), and long cycle life (600 cycles). The remarkable electrochemical properties of MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs could be explained by the following reasons: the enhanced electrical conductivity and the short pathway for Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion and electron transport derived from the 3D interconnected and conductive MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs networks. In addition, the expanded interlayer spacing (0.66 nm) of MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets accelerates Na<sup>+</sup> transport and the C–O–Mo chemical bonding facilitates charge transfer. The excellent electrochemical performance of MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs renders it great potential as an anode material for flexible SIBs.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.1.  Chemicals</h3><div class="NLM_p last"><i>N</i>,<i>N</i>-dimethylformamide (DMF, &gt;99.9%), sodium molybdate dehydrate (Na<sub>2</sub>MoO<sub>4</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O, ≥99%), <span class="smallcaps smallerCapital">l</span>-cysteine (HSCH<sub>2</sub>(NH<sub>2</sub>)CO<sub>2</sub>H, 99%), and molybdenum sulfide (MoS<sub>2</sub>, 98%) were purchased from Aladdin. Polyacrylonitrile (PAN, <i>M</i><sub>w</sub> = 150 000) was bought from Sigma-Aldrich.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.2.  Synthesis of Self-Standing and Flexible Carbon Nanofibers</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Self-standing and flexible CNFs were prepared by a facile single-electrospinning method. 0.9 g polyacrylonitrile was dissolved in 9.1 g <i>N</i>,<i>N</i>-dimethylformamide. The obtained mixed solution had been agitated for 12 h under ambient temperature. Afterward, the resultant homogeneous solution was poured into a 5 mL syringe with a spinneret of 16-gauge. The flow velocity, applied voltage, and distance between the spinneret and the aluminum foil collector were 35 μL min<sup>–1</sup>, 17 kV, and 20 cm, respectively. The obtained product was first treated at 250 °C for 3 h in air and followed with an annealed process at 1000 °C for 2 h with a ramping rate of 5 °C min<sup>–1</sup> under the atmosphere of Ar (denoted as CNFs).</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6">2.3.  Synthesis of Self-Standing and Flexible MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs</h3><div class="NLM_p last">First, a piece of CNFs (3 × 2 cm<sup>2</sup>) was soaked into HNO<sub>3</sub> for several hours to modify the surface with various oxygen-containing functional groups. The pretreated CNFs were rinsed by deionized water (DIW) and ethanol&nbsp; three times. The self-standing and flexible MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs was prepared through a facile hydrothermal method under the assistance of <span class="smallcaps smallerCapital">l</span>-cysteine. 90 mg Na<sub>2</sub>MoO<sub>4</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O and 360 mg <span class="smallcaps smallerCapital">l</span>-cysteine were dispersed in 30 mL ultrapure water under stirring. Then, CNFs and the above solution were added into a 50 mL Teflon-lined stainless-steel autoclave and kept at 220 °C for 12 h. The obtained product was washed by DIW and ethanol three times and further dried at 60 °C overnight. Finally, the MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs was obtained by annealing in Ar/H<sub>2</sub> (8%) at 800 °C for 2 h by a ramping rate of 1 °C min<sup>–1</sup> to obtain highly crystalline MoS<sub>2</sub>. MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets were prepared by <span class="smallcaps smallerCapital">l</span>-cysteine-assisted hydrothermal method which was similar to that for MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs, except the absence of CNFs.</div></div><div id="sec2_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7">2.4.  Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The material morphologies were observed by high-resolution scanning electron microscopy (HRSEM; SU8220, Hitachi) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM; JEM-2100F, JEOL). The X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns were taken from 2θ = 10° to 80° by X’pert Powder (PANalytical B.V.) using CuKα radiation. Thermal gravimetric analysis (TGA) was measured on TG 209F3 (NETZSCH) with a heating rate of 10 °C min<sup>–1</sup> in air. The Raman spectra were investigated using LabRAM Aramis with a laser wavelength of 532 nm. The X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS, Thermo Scientific Escalab 250Xi) analysis was measured with a monochromatic AlKα radiation of 1486.6 eV.</div></div><div id="sec2_5" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8">2.5.  Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">All the electrochemical measurements were tested by assembling 2032-type coin cells. Self-standing and flexible MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs and CNFs were directly used as anodes by cutting into a disk (8 mm). It was estimated that the mass loading of MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs in resultant electrode film was around 1.0 mg cm<sup>–2</sup> (8 mm disk, 0.50 mg). On the basis of the thickness of the film (18 μm), the volumetric density of flexible MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs is about 0.56 g cm<sup>–3</sup>. The films were ground into power to get its tap density of about 0.89 g cm<sup>–3</sup>. The bulk MoS<sub>2</sub> microparticles anodes were prepared by mixing the MoS<sub>2</sub> (purchased from Aladdin, 60 wt %), Super P (30 wt %), and PVDF (10 wt %) in several drops NMP into a homogeneous slurry, and subsequently coated onto a copper foil and further dried at 60 °C for 24 h. Sodium rolled into foil was utilized as the counter electrode. The electrolyte was composed of 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> dissolving in a mixed solution of ethylene carbonate and diethyl carbonate (EC/DEC, 1:1 by volume) with 5 wt % fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC), while the separator was Whatman glass microfiber filters (GF/F). The 2032 coin cells were prepared in an Ar-filled glovebox that maintained both the H<sub>2</sub>O and O<sub>2</sub> concentrations below 1.0 ppm. Electrochemical measurements were performed on a NEWARE battery testing system. The discharge/charge current density and related specific capacity were calculated on the gross mass of MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs and CNFs electrode, and the performance of bulk MoS<sub>2</sub> anode was evaluated based on the weight of MoS<sub>2</sub>. Besides, cyclic voltammograms were tested on Gamry Interface 1000E with a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> at a potential range of 0.05–3 V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na). Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) was collected by Gamry Interface 1000E in a frequency range of 1 MHz to 0.01 Hz.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i9">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs films were fabricated through an electrospinning method followed by hydrothermal treatment, giving rise to large, flexible films. Additionally, the flexible MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs shows high flexibility under rolling (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b05825/suppl_file/am8b05825_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a> of the <a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="Supporting Information">Supporting Information</a>, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b05825/suppl_file/am8b05825_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">SI</a>). The crystal structure of MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs was identified using X-ray diffraction (XRD). As can be seen in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>, the diffraction peaks could be well indexed to 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> with hexagonal structure (JCPDS 37-1492). It displays no impurity peaks from Mo or other impurities, indicating the formation of pure MoS<sub>2</sub>. The small peak around 2θ = 25° is attributed to amorphous carbon. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> Notably, the (002) peak of hexagonal 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> is supposed to exist at 2θ = 14.38° (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b05825/suppl_file/am8b05825_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>); the blue shift in the (002) diffraction (from 14.38° to 13.75°) is attributed to the expanded interlayer space of (002) plane of MoS<sub>2</sub>. Raman spectra of MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs, CNFs and commercial MoS<sub>2</sub> are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b05825/suppl_file/am8b05825_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>. MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs and CNFs both exhibit two peaks at 1355 and 1603 cm<sup>–1</sup>, originating from the respective D and G-band of carbon. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44 ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44 ref45" aria-label="reference 44 and 45">(44,45)</a> The peaks located at 382 and 409 cm<sup>–1</sup>, in line with the E <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1" aria-label="reference 1">(1)</a><sub>2g</sub> and A<sub>1g</sub> planes of MoS<sub>2</sub> with the hexagonal layered structure. In comparison to the commercial MoS<sub>2</sub>, MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs shows a shift of A<sub>1g</sub> plane from 409 to 406 cm<sup>–1</sup>, which confirms the fact that the crystalline interplanar spacing of MoS<sub>2</sub> was expanded. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46 ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46 ref47" aria-label="reference 46 and 47">(46,47)</a> The expanded interlayer space might be caused by the slow ramping rate during the calcined process together with coaxial structure which leads to undulated surface and expansion. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48 ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48 ref49" aria-label="reference 48 and 49">(48,49)</a> The mass percentage of MoS<sub>2</sub> in MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs was measured via thermal gravimetric analysis (TGA, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>). There is a small weight loss when the temperature below 100 °C caused by the evaporation of moisture on the composite. The weight loss between 100 and 500 °C would be attributed to two main reasons: (1) the oxidization of carbon in the composite in air and (2) the oxidation of MoS<sub>2</sub> to MoO<sub>3</sub> in this process. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref50 ref51'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50 ref51" aria-label="reference 50 and 51">(50,51)</a> The weight faction of remaining product MoO<sub>3</sub> is about 57%. Therefore, it is calculated that the content of MoS<sub>2</sub> in as-prepared MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs composite is as high as 63.1 wt % (the estimate formula is in Part I of the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b05825/suppl_file/am8b05825_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">SI</a>).</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b05825/asset/images/medium/am-2018-05825f_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b05825/asset/images/large/am-2018-05825f_0006.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. XRD pattern of MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b05825/asset/images/large/am-2018-05825f_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b05825&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b05825/asset/images/medium/am-2018-05825f_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b05825/asset/images/large/am-2018-05825f_0007.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. TGA curve of MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b05825/asset/images/large/am-2018-05825f_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b05825&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The morphologies of CNFs and MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs were observed using scanning electron microscope (SEM, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a, b). 1D CNFs display an average diameter of ∼280 nm, and the rough surface is caused by the decomposition of organic components of PAN. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref52'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52" aria-label="reference 52">(52)</a> From <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b05825/suppl_file/am8b05825_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>, it can be seen that these 1D nanofibers are constructed to a 3D interconnected and conductive nanofibers network. Afterward, MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets were epitaxially deposited on 1D CNFs by a hydrothermal reaction under the assistance of <span class="smallcaps smallerCapital">l</span>-cysteine. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> The SEM images show a 3D network assembled with uniform nanofiber coated with MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b05825/suppl_file/am8b05825_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>). In this synthesis process, <span class="smallcaps smallerCapital">l</span>-cysteine could not only release H<sub>2</sub>S as S source and reducing agent to generate MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets, but also create various functional groups (e.g., −SH and −COO<sup>–</sup>) for the conjugation of Mo ions to strengthen the adhesion between MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets and carbon nanofibers. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12 ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12 ref42" aria-label="reference 12 and 42">(12,42)</a> After annealing treatment under Ar/H<sub>2</sub> atmosphere at 800 °C, the MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs was obtained. As we can see from <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c, d and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b05825/suppl_file/am8b05825_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S6</a>, the 3D interconnect and conductive networks were well retained after annealing and MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets fully covered the surface of 1D CNFs. The average diameter of MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs is about 600 nm. The uniform 3D interconnected structure was well maintained after the hydrothermal process due to the strong mechanical strength of CNFs. Moreover, epitaxially grown MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets constructed nanovoids on the 1D CNFs, which in turn favors electrode/electrolyte interaction. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a></div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b05825/asset/images/medium/am-2018-05825f_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b05825/asset/images/large/am-2018-05825f_0001.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a, b) SEM images of CNFs at different magnifications; (c, d) SEM images of MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs at different magnifications.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b05825/asset/images/large/am-2018-05825f_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b05825&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To further understand the morphology and the structure of MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs, transmission electron microscopy (TEM) analysis was performed. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a illustrates a typical TEM image of MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs. It could be clearly observed that the MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets are densely grown on 1D carbon nanofibers and act as a building block of the hierarchical structure. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b, an enlarged TEM image suggests a thickness of about 150 nm for the MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets. The hierarchical MoS<sub>2</sub> structure was built by densely sheet-like MoS<sub>2</sub> subunits on the CNFs surface. The polycrystalline nature of MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs could be verified by a series of concentric rings in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b05825/suppl_file/am8b05825_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>. High-resolution images of representative 2D layered structure in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a> c, d reveal lattice fringes with an interplanar spacing of 0.66 nm, an enlarged (002) lattice planes of 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub>, which is in accordance with the results of XRD and Raman spectra (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a> and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b05825/suppl_file/am8b05825_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S3</a>). To further investigate the reason for the expanded MoS<sub>2</sub> layer, the interlayer spacing of various MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs samples which is obtained before annealing, annealed at 10 °C min<sup>–1</sup> and MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheet without CNFs annealed at 1 °C min<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, was investigated by HRTEM. The corresponding interlayer spacing is 0.66, 0.63, and 0.62 nm, respectively (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b05825/suppl_file/am8b05825_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S8–10</a>). Those results confirm that the expanded interlayer space might be caused by the slow ramping rate together with coaxial structure (detailed data and discussion seen in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b05825/suppl_file/am8b05825_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S8–S10</a>, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b05825/suppl_file/am8b05825_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">SI</a>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48 ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48 ref49" aria-label="reference 48 and 49">(48,49)</a></div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b05825/asset/images/medium/am-2018-05825f_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b05825/asset/images/large/am-2018-05825f_0002.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a, b) TEM images; (c, d) HRTEM images of MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs at different magnifications.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b05825/asset/images/large/am-2018-05825f_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b05825&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To investigate the interfacial features between CNFs and MoS<sub>2</sub> in the MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs films, X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) measurements were performed and analyzed. Overall, the survey XPS of the MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs composite reflects the existence of Mo, C, O, and S elements in the composites (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b05825/suppl_file/am8b05825_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a>). In the Mo 3d XPS region (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a), two peaks located at 232.6 and 229.4 eV, which could be ascribed to the binding energy of Mo 3d<sub>3/2</sub> and Mo 3d<sub>5/2</sub> of Mo<sup>4+</sup> in MoS<sub>2</sub>, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref53'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53" aria-label="reference 53">(53)</a> Small peak of 235.8 eV was the satellite peak of Mo<sup>6+</sup>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> Another peak at 226.6 eV is ascribed to S 2s. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9" aria-label="reference 9">(9)</a> The peaks located at 163.4 and 162.3 eV belong to the coexistence of S 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and S 2p<sub>3/2</sub> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref54 ref55'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54 ref55" aria-label="reference 54 and 55">(54,55)</a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c shows the C 1s spectra consisting of four peaks, which includes a main peak for C–C/C═C at 284.7 eV and three other peaks at 286.1, 286.4, and 288.5 eV, corresponding to C–O, C═O, and O–C═O, respectively. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d, the spectrum of O 1s, could be decomposed into four parts, 533.8 eV for C–OH, 531.2 eV for C–O, 530.7 eV for C═O, and 532.4 eV for the C–O–metal bond. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref56 ref57'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref56 ref57" aria-label="reference 56 and 57">(56,57)</a> According to the O 1s spectrum and the previous reports, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9 ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref39" aria-label="reference 9 and 39">(9,39)</a> it could be concluded that the peak at 532.4 eV in O 1s region should be attributed to the C–O–Mo bonds. Hence, MoS<sub>2</sub> and CNFs are combined by the C–O–Mo bonds, implying strong interaction between Mo species and functional groups on CNFs derived from the activation of HNO<sub>3</sub>, the multifunctional groups of <span class="smallcaps smallerCapital">l</span>-cysteine and the peroxidation and carbonation process of PAN-derived CNFs.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b05825/asset/images/medium/am-2018-05825f_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b05825/asset/images/large/am-2018-05825f_0003.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. XPS spectra of (a) Mo 3d, (b) S 2p, (c) C 1s, and (d) O 1s of MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b05825/asset/images/large/am-2018-05825f_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b05825&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To highlight the superior Na<sup>+</sup> storage properties of the MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs, the electrochemical performance of MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs and MoS<sub>2</sub> was comprehensively investigated and compared in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>. The typical CV curves of MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs for the first five cycles at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> within the potential window of 0.05–3 V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup> are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref58'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref58" aria-label="reference 58">(58)</a> Four clear anodic peaks can be observed in the first cycle at 1.2, 0.8, 0.6, and 0.05 V. Correspondingly, four oxidation peaks can be observed at 0.7, 1.7, 2.2, and 2.6 V. A good stability and reversibility of as-prepared electrode could be proven by the nearly coincident CV peaks in the following four cycles. The peak at 0.8 V decreased in the following cycles could be ascribed to the generation of solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) film during the first cycle, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10" aria-label="reference 10">(10)</a> leading to the large irreversible capacity loss and the low initial Coulombic efficiency during the initial charge/discharge processes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref59'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref59" aria-label="reference 59">(59)</a> The reduction peaks in 1.2 and 0.6 V are caused by the intercalation and the conversion process, respectively. The sodium storage mechanism of MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs could be explained by the following two reactions: <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref60'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref60" aria-label="reference 60">(60)</a></div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b05825/asset/images/medium/am-2018-05825f_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b05825/asset/images/large/am-2018-05825f_0004.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. (a) Cycle voltammograms of MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs; (b) galvanostatic charge and discharge curves of MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs; (c) rate performance of MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs and bulk MoS<sub>2</sub> microparticles; (d) cycling performance of MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs and bulk MoS<sub>2</sub> microparticles at a constant density of 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>; and (e) cycling performance of MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs at a large constant density of 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b05825/asset/images/large/am-2018-05825f_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b05825&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Intercalation:<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b05825/asset/images/medium/am-2018-05825f_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i10"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div></span></div><div class="NLM_p">Conversion:<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b05825/asset/images/medium/am-2018-05825f_m002.gif" alt="" id="_i11"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div></span></div><div class="NLM_p">The sharp curve around 0.05 V is a common phenomenon in conversion mechanism-based anode materials, which is caused by Na<sup>+</sup> inserting into the interface between Na<sub>2</sub>S and Mo. Furthermore, the almost overlaps of reduction and oxidation peaks from the second cycle demonstrate favorable reversibility and excellent cycling durability of Na<sup>+</sup> storage in MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs anode.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>b depicts  galvanostatic charge/discharge curves of MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. The Slopes at 0.9–1.25 V, 0.8–0.9 V, and 0.2–0.7 V match the result of the CV curves (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a) well. Besides, the overlapping of charge/discharge curves from second cycle shows the consistency with the CV results, which could also certify the considerable reversibility and excellent cycling durability of MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs anode. The rate capability of the MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs and bulk MoS<sub>2</sub> microparticles were shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>c. Apparently, the MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs manifests outstanding rate performance and high specific capacities compared to bulk MoS<sub>2</sub> microparticles. The MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs yields high reversible capacities of 578, 567, 539, 470, and 412 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at the current densities of 50, 100, 200, 500, and 1000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>c and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b05825/suppl_file/am8b05825_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S12</a>). Furthermore, the MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs could still reach an average capacity as high as 550 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> when the current density recovered to 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, manifesting superior rate capability. For comparison, the bulk MoS<sub>2</sub> microparticles exhibited reversible capacities of 451, 264, 165, 91, and 42 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at the current densities of 50, 100, 200, 500, and 1000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. After the current density recovered to 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, the bulk MoS<sub>2</sub> microparticles delivered an average capacity of about 200 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. The excellent rate behavior of MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs might be caused by the high conductivity of CNFs and the small transport resistance than bulk MoS<sub>2</sub> microparticles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b05825/suppl_file/am8b05825_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13</a>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The cycling durability and corresponding Coulombic efficiencies of MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs were investigated at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. As we could see from <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>d, the MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs delivered an initial discharge and charge capacity of 981 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> and 528 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> (293 mA h cm<sup>–3</sup>), respectively. Moreover, the initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) is 53.8%. The reasons of low ICE and irreversible capacity in the first cycle are usually caused by the generation of SEI on the surface of anode materials and the decomposition of electrolyte. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref61'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref61" aria-label="reference 61">(61)</a> Nevertheless, the Coulombic efficiency of MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs reached 90% from the second cycle and maintained more than 90% in the following cycles. After 100 cycles, the MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs retained a high reversible capacity of 438 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, indicating its low capacity decrease per cycle. For comparison, the bulk MoS<sub>2</sub> microparticles exhibited a specific capacity of 845 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> under the same current density of 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. After 100 cycles, the specific capacity faded rapidly (58 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>) because of the restacking and aggregating of bare MoS<sub>2</sub> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b05825/suppl_file/am8b05825_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S14</a>). Meanwhile, self-standing and flexible CNFs exhibited a much stable but lower capacity of 130 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b05825/suppl_file/am8b05825_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S15</a>), which approaches to the reversible capacity of other carbonaceous materials electrode for SIB like graphene. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref62'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref62" aria-label="reference 62">(62)</a> Considering the content of CNFs in the MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs composite materials (less than 40 wt %), CNFs contributes a specific capacity of 50 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at most. Therefore, MoS<sub>2</sub> is the major contributor of the high reversible capacity, which shows stable cycling stability than the fast capacity fading of bulk MoS<sub>2</sub> microparticles. The long-term cycle ability of MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs were tested at large current densities of 1000 mA g<sup>–1</sup> (1.0 mA cm<sup>–2</sup>)&nbsp;&nbsp;The MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs delivered a capacity of 282 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> or 0.28 mA h cm<sup>–2</sup> after 600 cycles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>e). The excellent cycling performance of flexible MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs electrode outperforms those previously reported MoS<sub>2</sub>-based anodes for SIBs (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b05825/suppl_file/am8b05825_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>), such as mPF-MoS<sub>2</sub>@G (200 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> or 0.24 mA h cm<sup>–2</sup>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref63'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref63" aria-label="reference 63">(63)</a> Ultrahigh Coulombic efficiencies of over 99% were achieved in large current density of 1000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, which shows obvious capacitive characteristics with sloping curves in the charge/discharge curves (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b05825/suppl_file/am8b05825_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12</a>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11" aria-label="reference 11">(11)</a> Notablely, the MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs electrode showed an increasing capacity in the first few cycles, which is attributed to the electrolyte molecules gradually penetrating into the interior electrode materials and the progressive electrochemistry active process in the film during the cycling. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref64'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref64" aria-label="reference 64">(64)</a> EIS results shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b05825/suppl_file/am8b05825_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S16</a> could also be another evidence for this phenomenon. Compared with the fresh coins, the impedance of MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs decreases due to the activation effect during the cycling after 200 cycles. What’s more, after 400 cycles, the impedance does not change obviously compared with that after 200 cycles, manifesting the activation effects. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a> Importantly, after cycling 100 cycles, the 3D interconnected and conductive networks of MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs were well kept, indicating the excellent structure stability of MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs for enhanced cycling performance (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b05825/suppl_file/am8b05825_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S17</a>). Hence, it is obvious that MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs work well as a flexible electrode for enhanced sodium storage performance.</div><div class="NLM_p last">The excellent cycling durability and superior rate performance of the MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs could be ascribed to the integrated strategy of unique rational-designed hybrid nanostructure and hybridized with conductive CNFs. Specifically, the 3D interconnected and conductive MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs networks with ultrathin 2D nanosheets possess a short pathway for Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion and electron transport and huge contact area between the electrode and the electrolyte, leading to enhanced rate capacity and cycle stability. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12 ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12 ref20" aria-label="reference 12 and 20">(12,20)</a> Besides, the expanded (002) planes of 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets with interlayer spacing of 0.66 nm could provide more active sites for electrochemical reaction and accelerate fast Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction and electron transport, which could efficiently enhances poor rate performance and limited capacity caused by their sluggish kinetics. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19 ref30 ref65'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19 ref30 ref65" aria-label="reference 19, 30 and 65">(19,30,65)</a> Meanwhile, flexible CNFs matrix can prevent the MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets from aggregation and improve electrical conductivity of the composite. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35 ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35 ref36" aria-label="reference 35 and 36">(35,36)</a> Furthermore, the C–O–Mo chemical bonding facilitates charge transfer, which also effectively improved the rate performance. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> Therefore, the synergy of all these features mitigates the problems of MoS<sub>2</sub>-based materials, assuring the enhanced Na<sup>+</sup> storage properties in SIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a></div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i12">4.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, we have developed a simple <span class="smallcaps smallerCapital">l</span>-cysteine-assisted hydrothermal approach to synthesize a self-standing and flexible 3D interconnected and conductive MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs by constructing hierarchical 2D MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets with enlarged interplanar spacing epitaxial grown on 1D electrospinning CNFs as a self-supported anode for SIBs. The MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets with expanded interlayer spacing epitaxially grew on the surface of 3D interconnected and conductive CNFs networks with C–O–Mo bonds. The C–O–Mo chemical bonding facilitates charge transfer and the expanded interlayer spacing of MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets accelerates Na<sup>+</sup> transport. The 3D interconnected and conductive MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs exhibited remarkable specific/volumetric capacity, outstanding rate performance, and excellent cycle stability as an anode for SIBs. Besides, flexible electrodes without binder, conductive additives, and metal current collectors in this study, which means a lighter overall electrode weight boosting the energy densities of the flexible electronics. This elaborate strategy offers a new thought to prepare high-performance energy storage materials, especially for flexible battery devices.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i13">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.8b05825" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.8b05825</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Estimated formula of TGA results and a comparison table of MoS<sub>2</sub>-based materials; digital photos of flexible CNFs and MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs; Raman spectra, XRD pattern, electrochemical impedance spectra, and SEM images of bulk MoS<sub>2</sub>; Raman spectra, SEM image, electrochemical impedance spectra, and cycling performance of CNFs; Raman spectra, SEM images, XPS Spectra, TEM images, SAED pattern, electrochemical impedance spectra, and galvanostatic charge/discharge curves of MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNFs (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b05825/suppl_file/am8b05825_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b05825/suppl_file/am8b05825_si_001.pdf">am8b05825_si_001.pdf (1.52 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.8b05825" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

T.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Charge Carriers in Rechargeable Batteries: Na Ions vs. Li Ions</span>. <i>Energy Environ. Sci.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2013</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>6</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">2067</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">2081</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/c3ee40811f</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D6%26publication_year%3D2013%26pages%3D2067-2081%26journal%3DEnergy%2BEnviron.%2BSci.%26author%3DS.%2BY.%2BHong%26author%3DY.%2BKim%26author%3DY.%2BPark%26author%3DA.%2BChoi%26author%3DN.%2BS.%2BChoi%26author%3DK.%2BT.%2BLee%26title%3DCharge%2BCarriers%2Bin%2BRechargeable%2BBatteries%253A%2BNa%2BIons%2Bvs.%2BLi%2BIons%26doi%3D10.1039%252Fc3ee40811f&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.8b05825&amp;doiOfLink=10.1039%2Fc3ee40811f&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Charge carriers in rechargeable batteries: Na ions vs. Li ions</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hong, Sung You; Kim, Youngjin; Park, Yuwon; Choi, Aram; Choi, Nam-Soon; Lee, Kyu Tae</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">6</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2067-2081</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1754-5706</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  We discuss the similarities and dissimilarities of sodium- and lithium-ion batteries in terms of neg. and pos. electrodes.  Compared to the comprehensive body of work on lithium-ion batteries, research on sodium-ion batteries is still at the germination stage.  Since both sodium and lithium are alkali metals, they share similar chem. properties including ionicity, electronegativity and electrochem. reactivity.  They accordingly have comparable synthetic protocols and electrochem. performances, which indicates that sodium-ion batteries can be successfully developed based on previously applied approaches or methods in the lithium counterpart.  The electrode materials in Li-ion batteries provide the best library for research on Na-ion batteries because many Na-ion insertion hosts have their roots in Li-ion insertion hosts.  However, the larger size and different bonding characteristics of sodium ions influence the thermodn. and/or kinetic properties of sodium-ion batteries, which leads to unexpected behavior in electrochem. performance and reaction mechanism, compared to lithium-ion batteries.  This perspective provides a comparative overview of the major developments in the area of pos. and neg. electrode materials in both Li-ion and Na-ion batteries in the past decade.  Highlighted are concepts in solid state chem. and electrochem. that have provided new opportunities for tailored design that can be extended to many different electrode materials for sodium-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqHBS0wIkomorVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljW0m99osmdCQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhtVWmtL%252FE&amp;md5=023136cc0e5ed94e18ce0d526a0000a4</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1016/j.ensm.2016.12.005"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cui, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yao, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Ever-growing energy needs and depleting fossil-fuel resources demand the pursuit of sustainable energy alternatives, including both renewable energy sources and sustainable storage technologies.  It is therefore essential to incorporate material abundance, eco-efficient synthetic processes and life-cycle anal. into the design of new electrochem. storage systems.  At present, a few existing technologies address these issues, but in each case, fundamental and technol. hurdles remain to be overcome.  Here the authors provide an overview of the current state of energy storage from a sustainability perspective.  The authors introduce the notion of sustainability through discussion of the energy and environmental costs of state-of-the-art Li-ion batteries, considering elemental abundance, toxicity, synthetic methods and scalability.  With the same themes in mind, the authors also highlight current and future electrochem. storage systems beyond Li-ion batteries.  The complexity and importance of recycling battery materials is also discussed.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGph4-d2B3Q8DLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liaS6zqiNbJbw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXhvFKlsrjF&amp;md5=977f0fc171bb41af30457715fc0ded72</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1039/C7TA00003K"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kang, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Recent Progress in Layered Metal Dichalcogenide Nanostructures as Electrodes for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Recent progress in layered metal dichalcogenide nanostructures as electrodes for high-performance sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kang, Wenpei; Wang, Yuyu; Xu, Jun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have emerged as a promising candidate for large-scale stationary energy storage in light of possible concerns over cost and abundance.  Advanced sodium host materials are required for developing SIBs with high energy d., long cycling stability and high safety.  Layered metal dichalcogenides (MX2, M = Mo, W, Sn, V, Ti; X = S, Se, Te) have become a hot spot for anode materials in SIBs due to their merits of high cond., mech. and thermal stability and structural stability.  In this review, we first present a comprehensive overview of the progress of layered MX2 anodes for SIBs.  Detailed discussion on the advantages of MX2 as SIB anodes is then made.  Emphasis is placed on enhancing the electrochem. performance through nanostructure engineering, crystal structure modulation, doping/alloying and composite design.  We conclude with a perspective on the further development of SIBs in view of their applications.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqJtS76pRrKJbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liaS6zqiNbJbw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXksFCksrs%253D&amp;md5=937107c9b1a2d8e6950bcbe9c948edd3</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1038/nchem.1589"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chhowalla, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shin, H.                         cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">The chemistry of two-dimensional layered transition metal dichalcogenide nanosheets</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Chhowalla Manish; Shin Hyeon Suk; Eda Goki; Li Lain-Jong; Loh Kian Ping; Zhang Hua</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature chemistry</span>

        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn"></span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">Ultrathin two-dimensional nanosheets of layered transition metal dichalcogenides (TMDs) are fundamentally and technologically intriguing.  In contrast to the graphene sheet, they are chemically versatile.  Mono- or few-layered TMDs - obtained either through exfoliation of bulk materials or bottom-up syntheses - are direct-gap semiconductors whose bandgap energy, as well as carrier type (n- or p-type), varies between compounds depending on their composition, structure and dimensionality.  In this Review, we describe how the tunable electronic structure of TMDs makes them attractive for a variety of applications.  They have been investigated as chemically active electrocatalysts for hydrogen evolution and hydrosulfurization, as well as electrically active materials in opto-electronics.  Their morphologies and properties are also useful for energy storage applications such as electrodes for Li-ion batteries and supercapacitors.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=LOZGTtr2ZcTRX3K9n3xrikBuFdjgVNKSfW6udTcc2eZ9x3oTuuJvibntkvexoksv"> &gt;&gt;                         cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">3D Interconnected and Multiwalled Carbon@MoS2@Carbon Hollow Nanocables as Outstanding Anodes for Na-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Yan; Qu, Qunting; Li, Guangchao; Gao, Tian; Qian, Feng; Shao, Jie; Liu, Weijie; Shi, Qiang; Zheng, Honghe</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Small</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">12</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">43</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">6033-6041</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">SMALBC</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1613-6810</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Currently, the specific capacity and cycling performance of various MoS2/C-based anode materials for Na-ion storage are far from satisfactory due to the insufficient structural stability of the electrode, incomplete protection of MoS2 by C, difficult access of electrolyte to the electrode interior, as well as inactivity of the adopted C matrix.  To address these issues, this work presents the rational design and synthesis of 3-dimensional interconnected and hollow nanocables composed of multiwalled C@MoS2@carbon.  In this architecture, (1) the 3-dimensional nanoweb-like structure brings about excellent mech. property of the electrode, (2) the ultrathin MoS2 nanosheets are sandwiched between and doubly protected by 2 layers of porous C, (3) the hollow structure of the primary nanofibers facilitates the access of electrolyte to the electrode interior, (4) the porous and N-doping properties of the 2 C materials lead to synergistic Na-storage of C and MoS2.  As a result, this hybrid material as the anode material of Na-ion battery exhibits fast charge-transfer reaction, high use efficiency, and ultrastability.  Outstanding reversible capacity (1045 mAh g-1), excellent rate behavior (817 mAh g-1 at 7000 mA g-1), and good cycling performance (747 mAh g-1 after 200 cycles at 700 mA g-1) are obtained.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo05Dvb9b6bubVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liaS6zqiNbJbw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhsVOqtrrN&amp;md5=c2c57e88989d1b427a457a951426780c</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201603812"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zou, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yousaf, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Han, R.                         cas_cit9R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">9</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">MoS2 Nanosheets Vertically Grown on Graphene Sheets for Lithium-Ion Battery Anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Teng, Yongqiang; Zhao, Hailei; Zhang, Zijia; Li, Zhaolin; Xia, Qing; Zhang, Yang; Zhao, Lina; Du, Xuefei; Du, Zhihong; Lv, Pengpeng; Swierczek, Konrad</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A designed nanostructure with MoS2 nanosheets (NSs) perpendicularly grown on graphene sheets (MoS2/G) is achieved by a facile and scalable hydrothermal method, which involves adsorption of Mo7O246- on a graphene oxide (GO) surface, due to the electrostatic attraction, followed by in situ growth of MoS2.  These results give an explicit proof that the presence of oxygen-contg. groups and pH of the soln. are crucial factors enabling formation of a lamellar structure with MoS2 NSs uniformly decorated on graphene sheets.  The direct coupling of edge Mo of MoS2 with the oxygen from functional groups on GO (C-O-Mo bond) is proposed.  The interfacial interaction of the C-O-Mo bonds can enhance electron transport rate and structural stability of the MoS2/G electrode, which is beneficial for the improvement of rate performance and long cycle life.  The graphene sheets improve the elec. cond. of the composite and, at the same time, act not only as a substrate to disperse active MoS2 NSs homogeneously but also as a buffer to accommodate the vol. changes during cycling.  As an anode material for lithium-ion batteries, the manufd. MoS2/G electrode manifests a stable cycling performance (1077 mAh g-1 at 100 mA g-1 after 150 cycles), excellent rate capability, and a long cycle life (907 mAh g-1 at 1000 mA g-1 after 400 cycles).</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqYLyG3o3maerVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh4UwadnYJVPQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhsVSgtbrM&amp;md5=9577fbdd77418cdf64d6e6ce1ae4d968</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit10" data-doi="10.1002/smll.201401521"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Luo, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gao, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Langrock, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mignerey, A.
